set of. local notch root strain measurements for a variety of load patterns in an Inconel 718 notch specimen at 649- C (1200- F)\nwas obtained and documented using the laser Interferometric Strain Displacement Gage (ISDG).\n\nThe ISDG was successfully adapted to the high-temperature\n\nmeasurements in this typical Ni-base superalloy and was shown to have a rela-\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\ntive uncertainty of \xc2\xb13% of the measured strain with an additional uncertainty of \xc2\xb1150 microstrain.\nMeasurements were made for\n\nS1X\n\nload patterns including continuous cyclic,\n\ncreep and cyclic with tensile and comprehensive hold periods on flat, doublenotch bars with an elastic stress concentration factor of 1.9.\nPedigree tensile and cyclic stress-strain data were also generated at\n649- C and employed in a simple Neuber analysis to obtain analytic predic-\n\ntions for comparisons to test results.\n\nA modified Neuber approach and a lim-\n\nited finite-element study were also compared to the data.\nA smooth bar specimen subjected to the notch root strain history recorded\nfrom a continuously cycled notch bar was also used to obtain stress behavior\ndata for comparison to the predictions.\nThe Neuber analysis predicted the first cycle notch root behavior very\nwell on the basis of hysteresis loop comparison when the notch root strain\nrate stress-strain response curve was used.\n\nThe stabilized cyclic loops were\n\nnot well predicted even when cyclic softening had stabilized.\n\nThe modified\n\nNeuber equation (corrected for stress redistribution due to plasticity) and\nthe finite-element analysis improved the cyclic correlation but did not totally resolve the problem.\nThe smooth bar test using the notch root strain history control shed additional light on this in that the maximum monotonic and stable cyclic stresses were well predicted by Neuber (as was the minimum stable cyclic stress)\nwhile the minimum monotonic (first cycle unload) was not.\n\nThis suggests that\n\nkinematic-hardening assumptions may be incorrect for the early cyclic transition period.\n\nThe utility of the computerized data acquisition and storage system\nassociated with the ISDG was demonstrated by examining cyclic history dependent parameters (e.g., loop area) on a cycle-by-cycle basis.\nwere used to assess data quality as well as behavioral\ntial for extension to life prediction application.\ntic loading slope\n\nw~s\n\nThese parameters\n\ntren~s,\n\nand have\n\npoten~\n\nThe hysteresis loop elas-\n\nused to predict notch root crack initiation which was\n\nconfirmed by posttest microscopy.\nThe program objectives of generation of benchmark notch data in a turbine disk alloy at elevated temperature and comparison to a Neuber analysis\nwere met.\n\nSignificant implications for future model development were deter-\n\nmined.\n\n,\n\n2\n\n2.0\n\n2.1\n\nINTRODUCTION\n\nTHE NEED FOR A BENCHMARK TEST\nAircraft gas turbine engine components are subjected to severe stress,\n\ntemperature, and environmental conditions.\n\nIn these components, local stress\n\nraisers (e.g., notches, boltholes, welds, fillet radii) are very often life\nlimiting areas in that low cycle fatigue failures generally initiate in these\ncritical regions.\n\nEconomic and reliability demands have prompted inordinate\n\neffort in development of analytic methods to first predict stresses and\nstrains in these complex geometry regions and, ultimately, predict the low\ncycle fatigue life for components containing these necessary design features.\nThese analytical developments have apparently been successful since numerous\ntechniques (most notably in the form of finite-element computer models) have\nevolved.\n\nThere remains, however, the need to check or verify these analytical\n\nmethodologies against actual experimental data measurements.\nsimple task.\n\nThis is not a\n\nMost stress concentration regions in gas turbine engines are\n\ngeometrically very small, eliminating many conventional extensometry methods\nfor strain measurement.\n\nFurther, conditions of interest include long times at\n\nelevated temperatures (near 649- C), eliminating conventional strain gage measurement methods.\n\nThe laser Interferometric Strain Displacement Gage was\n\nrecognized as having the potential to accomplish this demanding task and was\nemployed in this program.\nThe overall objective of this program was the generation, measurement,\nand documentation of the actual strains incurred at the root of a discontinuity in cyclically loaded test samples subjected to inelastic deformation at\nhigh temperature where creep deformations readily occur.\n\nA secondary objec-\n\ntive was to perform an analysis of the steady-state cyclic stress-strain\nresponse at the root of the discontinuity in the tested samples for comparison to the measured results.\nA nickel-base gas turbine disk alloy (Inconel 718) was chosen for this\nprogram; however, the analysis and measurement methodologies are directly\n,applicable to other materials as well, including blade and combustor alloys.\nAdditional needless complications arising from material instability,\n3\n\nanisotropy, and extensive localized oxidation damage were avoided by choosing Inconel 718.\n\nThese effects can be addressed in follow-on efforts.\n\nTest\n\ncycle elements known to be highly influential in complex cycle fatigue damage\nhave been included.\nData from this program provide a\n\nun~que\n\ndata base for ascertaining the\n\ndegree of correlation between measurements and available cyclic elasticplastic-creep finite-element analysis computer programs.\nThese results will substantially impact high-technology design\n\nwher~\n\nnotch fatigue life is the predominant component service life limitation and\nviable experimentally measured data have been lacking.\n2.2\n\nMORE GENERAL CONSIDERATIONS\nOne of the goals of solid mechanics is to enable the designer to predict\n\nthe behavior of complicated structures from the material properties determined in simple tests.\n\nThe most obvious example is design based on strength.\n\nGiven the material properties from a simple one-dimensional test, the designer\ncan predict failure of three-dimensional structures or components using the\ndistortion energy theory.\n\nA more recent example is fracture mechanics in\n\nwhich material properties determined from compact tension or three-point\nbending tests are used to predict fracture in complicated geometries.\nThe interplay between analytical and experimental solid mechanics is\nvividly demonstrated in the development of stress concentration factors as a\ndesign tool over 60 years ago.\n\nHere again, the elastic properties from one-\n\ndimensional tests are used in computing the strains in complicated geometries.\nThe analysis demonstrating the effect was available, but a new experimental\ntool - photoelasticity - was needed to verify that analysis and to determine\nstress concentration factors in components too complicated to analyze.\nThese same ideas carryover into design based on plastic yielding and\nalso on fatigue.\n\nThe Neuber relation is an analytical way of accounting for\n\nplastic yielding at stress concentrations, and it has been extended to fatigue\n.predictions at stress concentrations.\n\nAll of this work is in its infancy,\n\nhaving started approximately 15 years ago.\n4\n\nBut the subject of concern here -\n\nlow cycle fatigue behavior at stress concentrations at high temperature - has\nbarely been born.\n\nThe work reported herein is the first evaluation of the\n\nsimple Neuber approach and a sophisticated finite-element code using experimental data taken from a stress concentration at high temperature.\n\nIn analogy\n\ntophotoelasticity, a new experimental technique - the interferometric strain\ndisplacement gage - was required to provide the data.\n2.3\n\nREVIEW OF EXPERIMENTAL TECHNIQUES\nThe measuring of cyclic elastic-plastic strains at the root of a notch in\n\na high-temperature environment requires a versatile technique.\n\nThe technique\n\nmust be able to measure local strains in a high-strain gradient region.\n\nThis\n\nrequires a measuring system gage length which is small in comparison with the\nstrain gradient region.\n\nThe measuring technique must also be capable of mea-\n\nsuring strain at a rate rapid enough to accurately obtain the cyclic response\nof the specimen.\n\nThis requires either a continuous strain measuring device\n\nor the ability to measure at discrete points within a continuous load cycle.\nA third requirement of an acceptable measuring technique is that it cannot be\nadversely affected by a high-temperature environment.\nMonotonic elastic-plastic strains occurring at a stress concentration\nhave been accurately measured using a variety of techniques.\n\nA photodot tech-\n\nnique was employed by Merrill (Reference 1) to measure plastic strains occurring around a hole in a flat plate.\n\nA photoelastic coating method was utilized\n\nby Dixon (Reference 2) while Durelli and Sciammarella (Reference 3) used the\nMoire method.\n\nThese methods all yielded accurate results for the monotonic\n\ncase, but are not suited for the cyclic case.\nThe measuring of cyclic elastic-plastic strains occurring at a stress\nconcentration was first attempted by Griffith (Reference 4).\nmagnetic strain gages with a gage length of 12.7 mm (0.5 in.).\n\nHe used electroThis large\n\ngage length required the use of specimens which were 1473 mm x 610 mm (58 in. x\n24 in.) with a 102 mm (4 in.) diameter hole in the center to maintain a large\nratio between the size of the stress concentration and the gage length of the\nmeasuring system.\n\nThe accuracy of Griffith\'s measurements was considered to\n\n5\n\nbe within ~7.5i.. A similar large-scale specimen was used by Carroll, et al.\n(References 5, 6, 7). A specially designed strain transducer was used in\n"super-scale" aluminum specimens containing a 50 mm (2.0 inch) diameter center\nhole to monitor complex strain histories.\n\nCrews and Hardrath (Reference 8)\n\nemployed electric resistance strain gages with gage lengths of 1.59 mm\n(0.0625 in.).\n\nCrews reported a problem in maintaining the integrity of the\n\nbond between the gage and specimen throughout the duration of the test.\n\nLeis,\n\net al. (Reference 9), taking advantage of the improved technology in resistance\nstrain gages, employed gages with gage lengths of 0.38 mm (0.015 in.).\n\nThis\n\nsmaller gage allowed for the use of smaller specimens, but the problem encountered by Crews was still prevalent.\nThe interferometric strain displacement gage was utilized by Bofferding\n(Reference 10) to measure cyclic strains at notch roots.\n\nThis method, unlike\n\nthe resistance strain gage, does not measure strain continuously, but measures\nvery rapidly at discrete points within a continuous load cycle.\n\nThe ISDG has\n\na gage length which is typically 0.1 mm (0.004 in.) and is a noncontacting\nlaser-based measuring system.\nGuillot (Reference 11) extended the ISDG measuring system to measure\ncyclic elastic-plastic strains in notch roots of aluminum and steel specimens\nat 149\xc2\xb7 C (300\xc2\xb7 F) and 260\xc2\xb7 C (500\xc2\xb7 F), respectively.\nOne of the purposes of this study was to measure and document elasticplastic strains in the root of a notch under a variety of load patterns at a\ntest temperature of 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F).\n\nThe specimen material to be tested was\n\nInconel 718, a superalloy used in the aircraft turbine industry.\n\nThis was\n\naccomplished utilizing the ISDG measuring system previously developed.\n\nThe\n\nISDG is fully described in this report, as are the results from each test.\n2.4\n\nREVIEW OF ANALYTIC TECHNIQUES\nAnalytic determination of stresses and strains In stress concentration\n\nregions has been widely studied.\n\nOften, elastic stress concentration factors,\n\nKt (ratio of local stress to stress away from the concentration), have been\ndetermined for design use. For the case of purely elastic stresses (low Kt\n\n6\n\n..\n\nand/or applied load), closed form solutions of many notched, holed, and filleted structural geometries have been carried out by Neuber (Reference 12)\nand Savin (References 13 and 14).\n\nFinite-element computer techniques and\n\nphotoelasticity are also widely used.\n\nPeterson (Reference 15) has tabulated\n\nmany of these results in convenient graphical form.\nFor\n\nla~ger\n\nKt\'s and higher stresses, an elastic-plastic analysis that\n\naccounts for local notch root yielding is necessary since linear elastic\nassumptions no longer apply.\n\nNeuber (Reference 16) has presented an analyti-\n\ncal approximate means for determining local stresses and strains for cases\nbeyond the yield point.\n\nSince this method has been widely used (References\n\n17 through 20), it was selected for evaluation in this program and will be\ndiscussed in detail in this report.\n\nOther empirical methods such as the\n\nStowe11-Hardrath-Ohman (Reference 21) secant modulus approach have been used\nand modified (Reference 22) to obtain acceptable stress-strain predictions\nfor various cases.\n\nMore recently, however, elastic-plastic finite-element\n\nmethods have been employed to determine local plastic stress and strain fields\n(References 5, 23, and 24).\n\nBecause of modelling versatility, this approach\n\nappears to offer the most promise to provide a reliable means for expedient\nsolution of complicated notch geometry stress and strain distributions.\n\nSocie\n\n(Reference 25), fer example, has developed and demonstrated the viability of\na two-dimensional finite-element model that not only modeled cyclic notch root\nbehavior but also accounted for cyclic plasticity and residual stresses and\nstrains due to a growing fatigue crack.\nAnalytical predictions of notch root stress and strain for the specimens\nin this program were made using a Neuber-based model.\n\nSome additional analy-\n\nses utilizing an elastic-plastic-creep finite-element code (Reference 26) were\nalso made.\n2.5\n\nPROGRAM ORGANIZATION\nThis program combined the resources of the General Electric Company and\n\nLouisiana State University.\n\nThe General Electric Company utilized extensive\n\nanalytical capability and computational facilities to conduct the analytical\ntasks in the program.\n\nIn addition, based on extensive aircraft engine turbine\n\n7\n\ndisk alloy experience, a large material data base, and substantial material\nbehavior understanding, General Electric provided for the procurement of material and test specimen manufacture in accord with high aircraft engine industry standards.\nThe notched root strain measurement tasks were conducted by Dr. W.N.\nSharpe and Mr. M. Ward of Louisiana State University.\nated\n\n.\nminicomputer-controlled\n\nThe ISDG and associ-\n\nsystem for fringe motion monitoring are highly\n\nsuited for the measurement tasks in this program.\n\nThe measurement facilities\n\ndeveloped by Dr. Sharpe at LSU offered an excellent and highly viable approach\nto precisely measuring local notch root strains.\nThe approach consisted of a five-phase effort as shown diagrammatically\n~n\n\nFigure 1.\n\nTask I was a material and specimen procurement phase that\n\nincluded final detail design of a suitable double-notch specimen.\n\nIn Task\n\nII, the ISDG system was adapted to and checked against the measurement\nrequirements for the program.\n\nTask III was the testing phase wherein both\n\npedigree and benchmark notch tests were conducted.\n\nThe pedigree data were\n\nused to conduct a detailed modified Neuber notch analysis in Task IV.\n\nAnd\n\nfinally, measurements of notch root strain using the ISDG system were documented and compared to the analysis in Task V.\nformal report presentation is also shown.\n\n8\n\nA Task VI effort covering\n\nLSU\n\nGE\n\n.\n\n----- ----to. --- -l Task -II"!\'""\'I\n\nI\n1___\nI\nI\n\n"l.Ir--- - MATERIAL AND SPECIMEN CHARACTERIZATION\n\nIn this section the rationale for material\n\nchoi~e\n\nand the material and\n\nspecimen specifications is presented.\n3.1 MATERIAL SELECTION\nThe material used in this program was Inconel 718, a nickel-base superalloy widely used in current production gas turbine engines.\n\nThis alloy is\n\nprimarily used in turbine disk applications at temperatures approaching 649\xc2\xb7 C\n(1200\xc2\xb7 F).\nAn important material selection consideration, due to the objective of\nmeasuring highly localized strains, is microstructural homogeneity.\n\nA homog-\n\neneous and isotropic material is required in order that continuum mechanics\nanalyses can be evaluated against these benchmark experimental results.\nthese reasons, a fine, equiaxed grain size is needed.\n\nFor\n\nThermal mechanical pro-\n\ncessing parameters were available for Inconel 718 which would result in the\ndesired microstructure.\n\nConsideration of the gage length of the strain mea-\n\nsurement system indicated that an ASTM 9-11 grain size was desirable.\n\nThis\n\nresults in 6-12 grains on the average in the gage length.. This grain size is\nachievable in Inconel 718, and the material used in this study was processed\nto obtain this microstructure.\n\nIn an effort to avoid material waste, bar stock was used as the test\nspecimen material form.\n\nThis material was heat treated to simulate disk\n\nforging properties.\nSixteen hundred and thirty-eight newtons (368 lb) of Inconel 718 25.4\n(1.0 in.) 00 centerless ground bar stock material were received from ATEK\nMetals Company, Woodlawn, Ohio.\nSl08.\n\nThe material is from Teledyne ALLvac Heat No.\n\nVendor-supplied composition and certification test results are listed\n\nin Table I along with specification minimum and typical average properties.\nThe material is well within specification and appears to have approximately\naverage mechanical properties.\n\n10\n\n~\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTable I.\n\nVendor-Supplied Certification Properties.\n\n!nconel 71S, 25.4 mm (1.0 in.) diameter centerle\xe2\x80\xa2\xe2\x80\xa2 &round bar stock Teledyne ALLvac\nhaat No. 5108, Spec. !50TF15AS-10, AIEX No. AXS02370\na.\nElement\nAl\n!\n\nC\nCb+Ta\nCo\nCr\nCu\nFa\nb.\nSCNrca\nVendor\nSpec.\nTyp. TESTING\nTo assess material pedigree, a series of standard tensile and rupture\n\ntests was conducted.\n\nIn addition, to acquire data needed for notch strain\n\nanalysis, cylic stress-strain curve data were also obtained.\n3.2.1\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nTensile and Rupture Testing\n\nTensile and creep-rupture pedigree assessment specimens were manufactured to the dimensions and specifications shown in Figure 3.\n\nThe specimens\n\nwere manufactured by Metcut Research Associates, Cincinnati, Ohio.\n\nThe tests\n\nwere conducted in the Material and Process Technology Laboratories at General\nElectrlc.\nTable IV tabulates the results of four tensile tests (two each at 21- C\nand\n\n649~\n\nC) and four creep-rupture tests (all at 649-C).\n\nAlso shown in the\n\ntable for comparison are average standard results for forged\'Inconel 718.\nAt 21- C, the average results of the two tensile pedigree tests agree\nfairly well with average forging properties.\n\nYield strength is about 40 MPa\n\n(6 ksi) lower and ultimate strength about 20 MPa (3 ksi) higher than average.\nDuctility for the benchmark material is moderately higher.\nAt 649- C, the benchmark material is 13-55 MPa (2-8 ksi) higher in\nstrength level and much more ductile (by nearly a factor of two) than standard material.\n\nThis is thought to be the result of the very fine (ASTM 10)\n\ngrain size in the bar stock.\nCreep rupture results from Table IV are also plotted in Figure 4 for a\ncomparison to a standard curve.\n\nThe results indicate essentially average\n\nrupture life at 649- C (1200- F).\nThe creep behavior of the four specimens\nof creep strain versus time.\n\n~s\n\nshown in Figure 5 on a plot\n\nThese results are further detailed and compared\n\nto previous (standard) data in Figures 6, (CSOTF6).\n\n\'0\n\n....\ntil\n\n3.2.2\n\nCyclic Stress-Strain Curve Generation\n\nSix cyclic stress-strain tests using longitudinal strain control low\ncycle fatigue test specimens (Figure 9) were used to characterize the\ncyclic material response.\n\nThe specimens were manufactured and tested by\n\nMetcut Research Associates.\nThe cyclic stress-strain curve is of interest because many materials\nalter their deformation behavior after repeated loading.\nharden, soften, or remain neutral.\n\nThe material can\n\nThe bulk of the change is estimated to\n\ntake place in approximately the first 20% of life, with only small changes\ntaking place after that (Reference 27).\n\nHowever, for convenience and unifor-\n\nmity, the stable cyclic curve is usually defined at half life, Nf/2.\nThere is, however, no universal agreement on the exact definition of\nthe cyclic stress-strain curve nor on the test procedure to obtain it.\n\nThe\n\nmost common technique involves plotting the locus of the tips of the stable\nstress-strain loops from several specimens subjected to different levels of\ncompletely reversed constant strain amplitudes.\n\nBecause of the estimated\n\nrapid achievement of steady-state strain conditions, test efficiency can be\nimproved by cycling each specimen to several levels of applied strain; this\n1S\n\nshown in Figure lOa.\nAn alternate technique is shown in Figure lOb, where a triangular strain\n\ncycle with constant maximum strain is combined with a varying maximum strain\nto obtain a triangular wave shape with an increasing-decreasing amplitude.\nAnother possible approach is to halt any of the cyclic tests at specimen half\nlife and conduct a monotonic test.\nIt should be noted that the cyclic techniques are all based on testing\n\n-\n\nexperience at Rs = -1 (Rs = minimum strain/maximum strain). In an Rs = -1 test,\nthe material undergoes fully reversed loading about a mean strain of zero,\nresulting in load-displacement records that are essentially axisymmetric about\nthe origin.\n\nThe specimens do frequently develop a small, less than 34 MPa (5\n\nksi), compressive mean stress, however.\n\nPotential mean stress effects under\n\nother conditions were recognized by recommendations that multiple strain level\ntests be\n\nconduct~d\n\nusing a high-to-low load sequence to eliminate the mean\n\nstress.\n23\n\n5.000\n\n\xc2\xb1.O.\n\nHVISIONS\nOESC~,P-ON\n\nLTII\n\nDO. the\n\nincreasing-decreasing blocks of Figure lOb are used, then,for R\xe2\x82\xac\nentire cyclic curve can be obtained from a single test.\nconditions frequently do not correspond to R\xe2\x82\xac\ncurrent LCF work is conducted under R\n\nE:\n\n=0\n\n= -1\n\n= -I,\n\nthe\n\nBecause engine disk\n\nconditions, much of the\n\nconditions.\n\nUnlike R\xe2\x82\xac\n\n= -1,\n\nthe\n\nhysteresis loops obtained during a constant strain amplitude test and the loop\nat the corresponding strain range in the increasing-decreasing test mode are\nnot the same.\nIn other words, this means that even when the increasing-decreasing amplitude technique is used, the entire cyclic curve cannot be obtained from a\nsingle test for R\xe2\x82\xac\n\n= O.\n\nAt most, four or five points can be obtained from\n\na multiple strain level test (Figure lOa) and only a single point on the curve\nis obtained when a constant maximum strain range is used.\n\nIf we define the\n\ncyclic (or loop) amplitude as half the total stress range of the hysteresis\nloop, the cyclic amplitude can be determined as a function of strain range,\nregardless of the test condition.\n\nIt should be independent of R ratio, as\n\nit describes the cyclic hardening/softening which is a material property.\n\nOn\n\nthe other hand, the mean stress, as has been indicated, depends on the test\ncondition.\n\nThus the cyclic stress-strain curve, as defined by the loop tips,\n\ndepends on the R ratio .\n....\'\nCook (Reference 28) has defined the "complete" cyclic stress-strain curve\nas the locus of the hysteresis loop tips rather than the loop amplitude which\nincludes the influence of mean stress.\nFor this program, recognizing a dependence on strain rate, tests were run\nfor R\xe2\x82\xac = -1 and R\xe2\x82\xac = 0 at controlled strain rates of 5%, 20%, and 100%/\nminute. All tests were done at 649- C. An incremental step-loading procedure,\nwherein. an initial strain level is imposed, the specimen cycled to approximately one-quarter of its life, a second (higher) level is imposed, the specimen cycled to one-half life and so on to failure, was employed.\nwere used in each test with a triangular waveform.\n\nFour steps\n\nFor each step, stress and\n\nstrain ranges were determined on the most nearly stabilized hysteresis loop in\nthe block.\n26\n\nIt was anticipated that stabilization would occur in the allotted\n\nblock times.\ndid occur.\n\nThe first step was the only one, however, where stabilization\nIn the other steps, the loops were nearly stable but were contin-\n\nuing to show small changes when the next block was started.\nMonotonic stress-strain curves were also obtained at strain rates of\nl%/minute and 20%/minute.\nA summary of the stress-strain data obtained for this program 1S given in\nTable V.\nFigure 11 shows the stress-strain response 1n terms of alternating stress\nand alternating plastic strain.\n\nAssuming a relation:\n\nthe A and n values 1n Table V were determined.\n\nIn general the slope of these\n\nFor the 20%/minute, Re = -1, curve\nthe lowest stress-strain point appears to be in error (at least 1n relation\n\ncurves is near the expected value of\nto the other data trends).\n\n~O.l.\n\nThis point has been ignored in constructing the\n\ncurves of Figure 11.\nFigure 12 compares cyclic stress-strain curves based on hysteresis\nloop amplitudes for these strain rates with Re = -1. Consistent data trends\nare observed.\nA strong strain rate dependence is shown with the higher rates\nhaving lower stress for a given strain.\nComparisons between the various curves are also shown in Figure 13.\nThere is considerable scatter as evidenced by comparing the two Re = 0\ncyclic curves. The complete stress strain curves represent the maximum\nstress and strain points in the hysteresis loops.\n\nThe trend of the complete\n\ncurve towards the cyclic curve is typical and illustrates that for small\nstrain there is a stable mean stress present.\nFor reference, detailed test data are presented 1n Table VI.\n\n27\n\nTable V.\n\nType( 1)\n\nCyclic Stress-Strain Curve Parameters, Incone1 718, Heat Treated to Forging Specifications,\n649 0 C (1200 0 F).\nA(2)\nRatio\n\nR\n\nRatio\n\n.\n\n%/min\n\nA(4)\n\nEO)\n\nE:\n\nX10 3 MPa\n\nX10 6 psi\n\nX103 MPa\n\nksi\n\n1.4\n\nn(4)\n\nCc(S)\n\n202.3\n\n0.108\n\n0.986\n\n157.6\n(188.7)\n\n0.083\n(0.110)\n\n0.993\n(0.995)\n\nC\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n-1\n\n5.0\n\n155\n\n22.5\n\nC\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n-1\n\n20.0\n\n165\n\n23.9\n\nC\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n-1\n\n100.0\n\n158\n\n22.9\n\n1.1\n\n156.6\n\n0.092\n\n0.995\n\nC\n\n1.0\n\n0\n\n20.0\n\n165\n\n24.0\n\n1.4\n\n196.6\n\n0.121\n\n0.998\n\nC\n\n1.0\n\n0\n\n20.0\n\n164\n\n23.8\n\n1.4\n\n197.7\n\n0.106\n\n0.999\n\nM\n\n---\n\n---\n\n1.0\n\n161\n\n23.4\n\n1.2\n\n166.9\n\n0.054\n\n0.997\n\nM\n\n---\n\n---\n\n20.0\n\n164\n\n23.8\n\n1.4\n\n210.0\n\n0.063\n\n0.995\n\n1.1\n(1.3)(6)\n\n(l)M-Monotonic; No. 24) \xc2\xa3 = 20i./min\n1\n\nLoad\nOnly\n\n---\n\n943.2\n\nI\n\n3.2.3\n\nElastic Modulus\n\nIn order to assess measured notch root strains in the elastic regime, it\nis necessary to have accurate modulus of elasticity (E) values.\n\nAn under-\n\nstanding of the potential variability in measured E values is also important\nfor separating strain measurement errors from material property variations.\nTable VII summarizes the values of E obtained from the cyclic stress-strain curve tests and shows comparisons to Standard Inconel 718 forging dynamic E\nvalues.\n\nThe moduli for the benchmark material tests agree well with standard\n\nforsing dynamic moduli.\n3.3\n\nNOTCH SPECIMEN DESIGN\nThe benchmark notch specimen design for this program is shown in Figure\n\n14.\n\nThis design was the culmination of considerations that included the need\n\nfor:\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAn elastic stress concentration factor (K t ) representative of typical engine components - Kt approximately 1.9\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nA notch geometry that permits a clear path for incoming and reflected\nlaser beams - a shallow surface notch\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nA cross-sectional area compatible with the load cell capacity and\nthe need to obtain plasticity and creep behavior at the notch root\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nAdequate buckling restraint to allow substantial compressive loading\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nApproximate plane stress conditions at the notch root so as to provide for uniaxial straining in the measurement direction - thin gage\nsection\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSufficient length to provide clearance between the grip faces for a\nfurnace with optical ports for the laser beams - 254 mm (10 in.)\nlong\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nPrecise specimen alignment - buttonhead design\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nMinimal material cost and waste - bar stock, round gr1p ends.\n\nThe flared gage section evolved with the need to obtain the highest Kt\nfrom the 25.4 tom bar stock while preserving the shallow notch geometry. There\nwas also a desire to keep the mass at the ends of the specimen as low as possible to avoid undue bending of the thin gage section during handling.\nspecimens were shipped in plastic tubes as a further precaution.\n\n34\n\nThe\n\n..\n\nTable VII.\n\nComparison of Elastic Modulus (E) Measurements Incone1 718, Bar, Heat Treated to Forging Spec.\n(x 10 9 Pa [x 10 6 psi]).\n\nII\n\n\xc2\xa3\n\nTest(l)\n\n(%/min)\n\nlatio\n\n21\' C\n(70\' F)\nht Cycle\n\n649\' C\n(1200\' F)\nlit Cycle\n\n649\' C\n(1200\' F)\nNf/2\n\n1\n\n5.0\n\n-1\n\n201.3\n(29.2)\n\n155.8\n(22.6)(3)\n\n155.1\n(22.5)\n\n2\n\n100.0\n\n-1\n\n198.6\n(28.1)\n\n157.0\n(22.78)\n\n157.6\n(22.85)\n\n3\n\n20.0\n\n-1\n\n203.4\n(29.5)\n\n162.9\n(23.63)\n\n164.4\n(23.85)\n\n4\n\n20.0\n\n0\n\n204.1\n(29.6)\n\n164.7\n(23.81)\n\n165.5\n(24.00)\n\n5\n\n20.0\n\n0\n\n207.5\n(30.1)\n\n164.4\n(23.15)\n\n164.3\n(23.13)\n\n6\n\n1.0\n\n204.1\n(29.7)\n\n161.3\n(23.4)\n\n-\n\n7\n\n20.0\n\n164.1\n(23.8)\n\n-\n\n-\n\n--\n\n-\n\nAva\xc2\xb7\n\n203.3\n(29.41)\n\n161.5\n(23.42)\n\n161.4\n(23.41)\n\nStd. to be made from Peterson (Reference 15), the importance\nof this value in the Neuber analysis necessitated the inclusion of an elastic\nfinite-element analysis of the final design.\nThree finite-element models were employed.\nwere run at General Electric.\nrun at LSU.\n\nTwo three-dimensional models\n\nA third, independent two-dimensional model, was\n\nThe General Electric models will be described first followed by\n\ncomparison to LSU results.\n\nThe first model was a three-dimensional model of\n\nthe specimen gage section only, Figure 15.\n\nThis model was of 1/8 of the gage\n\nsection, recognizing the three-fold symmetry.\n\n~~en\n\ninitial notch measure-\n\nments were made, they suggested that the gage section only model inadequately\ndescribed the stress distribution resulting from the flared specimen design.\nThe second model was therefore expanded to include the entire flat-toround transition section as shown in Figure 16.\nwas modeled.\n\nAgain, 1/8 of the specimen\n\n(It was later determined that in the initial tests the hardness\n\ntester indentations for the ISDG were not centered at the notch root which led\nto inaccurate measurements.\n\nThe adequacy of the first mesh was subsequently\n\nverified by the second model.)\nThe models used eight-noded isoparametric brick elements in the CYANIDE\nelastic-plastic-creep computer code.\n\nA nominal net section stress of 689.5\n\nMPa (100 ksi) was applied to both models.\n\n37\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nFigure 15.\n\n38\n\nFirst Mesh of Gage Section Only\n\nThree-Dimensional Finite-Element Model of\n1/8 of the Benchmark Notch Fatigue Specimen.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nSecond Mesh Including Transition Region\n\nFigure 16.\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThree-Dimensional Finite-Element Mesh Pattern\nof 1/8 of the Benchmark Notch Fatigue Specimen \xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n_."~~_._.....\n\n39\n\n..\n\n.\n\n._.~~~\n\n~_~_ _~\n\n.;...\n\n""-\n\n.......__ _ n......._,-IiI\xc2\xb7......_\n\xc2\xb7 ..... \xc2\xb7 \xe2\x80\xa2\n..\n\n..._ \'......_ \'........._ _1\n\'\xe2\x80\xa2\n. , ,,\n\nThere are several means of determining Kt from the finite-element\nresults. Calculated stress and strains are output at element centroids.\n\nBy\n\nplotting element centroid stresses versus distance from surface t an extrapolation to the specimen surface can be made.\n\nThis is done in Figure 17 for\n\nresults from the model of only the gage section.\n\nAn extrapolated value of\n\nKt \xe2\x80\xa2 1.94 is predicted compared to Peterson\'s 1.9. Since there are uncertainties in the extrapolation procedure t it is possible to employ actual element centroid stresses for Kt determination, providing the element size is\nsmall. The determination of a sufficiently small size is, however, again\nproblematical.\n\nOne can also estimate Kt by using surface nodal point displacements to calculate surface stress. Table VIII summarizes the various\nKt\'s obtained by applying these methods to the two models.\n\nThe two models\n\npredict consistent Kt\'s when extrapolated to the surface; however, the more\ncoarse elements in the second model place the centroids further from the surface predicting lower Kt\'s for the other methods.\nFigure 18 shows the predicted axial stress distribution across the specimen thickness (Modell, gage section only).\n\nA very small through-thickness\n\nvariation at the notch surface is shown, confirming an essentially plane\nstreas specimen design.\nAn independent two-dimensional finite-element code (developed at LSU)\nwas also used to compare to the above results.\n\nFigure 19 shows excellent cor-\n\nrelation among the different approaches on an axial strain versus distance\nfrom notch root graph.\nFinally, as also shown in Figure 19, strain measurements using\n\nres~s\xc2\xad\n\ntance strain gages (RSG) applied across the minimum section of the specimen\nconfirmed the basic trends.\n\nGages 0.79 mm (0.031 in.) long by 0.81 mm (0.032\n\nin.) wide, spaced 2.03 mm (0.08 in.) on centers were applied across the full\nnet section.\n\nAs the notch root was approached the RSG gave low results, prob-\n\nably due to an averaging over a relatively large area by the RSG.\nAccepting the finer mesh of the original model (Modell) as the best\nmodel and recognizing accuracy limitations, a value of Kt = 1.9 was accepted\nfor the benchmark specimen and used for all subsequent data reduction.\n\n40\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nDistance to Surface, in.\n\no\n\n1400\n\n0.1\n\n....1 - STRAIN MEASUREMENT SYSTEM ADAPTATION/DEVELOPMENT\n\nThe interferometric strain displacement gage measuring system was 1n\nplace at LSU and being used for similar fatigue strain measurements on an\nNSF-sponsored program.\n\nTwo substantial improvements to the system were accom-\n\nplished on the current NASA program - an upgrade of the fringe measuring equipment and development of a new measurement strategy.\n\nThe old fringe measuring\n\nequipment consisted of photomultiplier tubes (PMT) over 10 years old and homemade amp 1 ifiers.\ntrolled.\n\nAlso, the servocontrolled mirrors were not temperature con-\n\nBoth of these problems were solved with the purchase of new tubes,\n\nmlrrors, mirror control units, and amplifiers.\n\nThe new strategy switched from\n\nscanning several fringes to following the minimum of a single fringe; this\nincreased the testing rate by a factor of 2.5.\nThis section describes the system as it now exists and as it was applied\nto these particular specimens.\n\nThe basics of the ISDG are first briefly pre-\n\nsented and then followed by a detailed system description.\n\nDetails of the\n\ninstrumentation capabilities are given in the remaining sections which include\nsome preliminary tests.\n\n4.1\n\nBASICS OF THE ISDG MEASUREMENT SYSTEM\nThe ISDG is a laser-based measuring system which measures relative dis-\n\nplacement between two small indentations.\na Vicker\'s hardness tester.\n\nThese indentations are applied with\n\nThe indentations generally measure from 15 to 25\n\nmicrons on a side with center-to-center spacing ranging from 50 to 400 microns.\nFigure 20 shows a typical set of indentations.\nThe indentations are illuminated with highly coherent monochromatic laser\nlight, causing two diffraction patterns to form.\n\nThese diffraction patterns\n\noverlap, creating interference fringe patterns on either side of the laser\nbeam, Figure 21.\nfringes move.\n\nAs the distance between the indentations (d) changes, the\n\nThe phase difference between two interfering rays of light 1S\n\nd*sina, where a is the angle between the incident laser beam and the rays 1n\nquestion.\n\nWhenever the following relation is satisfied,\n\n45\n\nFigure 20.\n\nPhotomicrograph of Indentations in Root of\nNotch. The basic system\nconsists of a minicomputer in communication with various analog devices. A\nschematic of the system is shown in Figure 22.\nThe methodology followed by this measurement system is as follows:\n\nAs\n\nthe fringe patterns move in correlation with the relative displacement of the\nindentations, a predetermined minimum on each channel is tracked by the computer. This is accomplished by sending a computer-generated, 6~-step ramp to\n48\n\n,\n\nPhotomultiplier\n\\ ) fube\n\nServocontrolled\nMirror\n\nMinicomputer\n\nLaser\n\nD/A Converter\nAID Converter\n\nInverter\nAmplifier\n\nspeCim~\n\nFigure 22.\n\nSchematic of the Minicomputer-Controlled ISDG System.\n\nthe servocontroller. causing each mirror to rotate about its\n\nax~s.\n\nThe total\n\nangular rotation of the mirror is adjusted via the gain control on the servocontroller to scan only the "trough" region surrounding the predetermined\nminimum of each fringe pattern.\n\nThe fringe pattern is swept over a narrow\n\nslit over the photomultiplier tube face.\n\nFigure 23 shows a typical fringe pat-\n\ntern with the trough region identified.\nthe mirrors constituting a sweep. the\n\nAt each one of the 60 increments of\n\nphotom~tiplier\n\ntubes sample the fringe\n\nintensities and relay their electric analogs to the minicomputer via an inverting amplifier. ,Typically. the minicomputer averages the 60 intensities recorded\nper channel with a six point sliding average routine to mask any noise present\nand then locates the new minimum intensity locations for each channel using a\nsimple comparison loop.\nnew minimum.\n\nThe next mirror sweep will now be centered about this\n\nThe total fringe displacement is determined by subtracting this\n\nnew minimum location from the original minimum location prior to any load on\nthe specimen.\n\nThe total fringe displacement is then multiplied by the appro-\n\npriate constant to determine strain.\nseconds to !enerate 1 data point.\n\nThis entire process takes 100 milli-\n\nNormally. 60 data points are gathered per\n\ncycle; alleviated.\nremains:\n\nOne problem, however,\n\nsurface oxidation occurring at high temperatures can effectively blot\n\nout the indentations, causing the required fringe patterns to become obscure.\nThis phenomenon is apparent in Inconel 718 at approximately 593\xc2\xb7 C (1100\xc2\xb7 F).\nSince the test measurements were to be made at 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F), a surface\npreparation method was devised which extended the measurable temperature range\nadequately.\n\nThe procedure is quite simple.\n\nThe notch root is first polished\n\nwith 600-grit sandpaper in a direction parallel with the major axis of the\nspecimen.\n\nThe specimen is then cleansed with soap and water, acetone, and\n\nmethanol, respectively.\n\nOnce the specimen has been polished and cleaned, it\n\nis placed in the furnace and heated at 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F) for approximately 30\nminutes.\n\nWhen the specimen is removed from the furnace, a thick oxide layer\n\nwill have formed in the notch root, as shown in Figure 30.\n\nThe notch root is\n\nnow lightly buffed with a wet felt wheel to remove most of the oxide, but not\nall of it.\n\nThis last step is quite crucial because a thin oxide layer seems\n\nto "sea1." the surface prohibiting any further oxide formation, while a thick\noxide layer is nonreflective.\n\nAfter buffing the notch root to the desired\n\neffect, the indentations are embedded 100 microns (0.004 in.) apart in the\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nroot of the notch using a Vicker\'s hardness tester with a vernier traveling\nbase.\n\nA photomicrograph of a prepared specimen prior to testing is shown in\n\nFigure 31.\n\n61\n\n. . \'\'\'-.\n\n~,\n\nsox\nFigure 30.\n\n62\n\nPhotomicrograph Showing Heavy Oxide in\nRoot of Notch After Preoxidation of\n649 0 C (1200 0 F).\n\nFigure 31.\n\nPhotomicrograph Showing Root of Notch After\nLight Polish and Indent Application. 200X.\n\n63\n\n4.5\n\nPRELIMINARY TEST RESULTS\nPrior to testing these\nare described in detail ln Section 5.1.\n\nHowever, it was felt that all "bugs"\n\nin the measured system had been located and corrected after tests using three\ntypical load patterns:\n\ncontinuous tension-compression, cyclic with 2-minute\n\ntension hold, and creep.\n\nA problem which was unveiled during Test No. OA was related to the large\namount of rigid body motion resulting from the relatively high tensile load\napplied to the specimen.\n\nThe rigid body motion occurring under a 35,600 N\n\n(8000 lb) tension load moved the two indentations out of the effective diameter of the laser beam, thus Obliterating the necessary fringe patterns.\nproblem was solved by moving the laser farther away from the specimen.\n64\n\nThis\n\n.\n\nThis allowed the laser beam to disperse to an effective diameter approximately\ntwo times the previous diameter.\n\nThe intensity of the laser beam striking the\n\nindentations was reduced by this solution, but the remaining intensity was\nstill large enough to produce the PMT signals which could be amplified to an\nacceptable range.\nTest No. OB through Test No.3 were all successfully conducted.\n\nHow-\n\never, a peculiar characteristic was noticed when the data were analyzed.\n\nAll\n\nof the strain data recorded seemed to be lower than expected, even the elastic\nstrain values.\nor procedure.\n\nIt was concluded an error was present in the measuring system\nUpon close inspection of Specimen No.5, it was noted that the\n\nindentations were not placed at the root of the notch, Figure 32.\n\nA further\n\ninvestigation of all the specimens revealed this to be a common problem.\n\nAs\n\na result of these misplaced indentations, the stress concentration factor utilized in analysis (K t\n\n= 1.9)\n\nwas inappropriately high; thus the strain data\n\nrecorded was less than that predicted.\n\nThis problem was alleviated prior to\n\nthe benchmark tests by a more careful indentation application procedure.\n\nA\n\nphotomicrograph of the notch region of each benchmark specimen was also taken\nafter applying the indentations to confirm their location at the root of the\nnotch.\n4.6\n\nMEASUREMENT SYSTEM EVALUATION\nAn error analysis of the strain measured by the ISnG for the notched\n\nspecimen at 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F) is conducted below.\nRecall that A in Equations 1 through 3 is the wavelength of the laser\nlight and thus introduces no error to the measurement theory; d is the distance between the two indentations.\nusing the microscope available.\n\nThis can be measured to within 0.2 micron\n\nSince 100 microns is a typical value for d,\n\nthis imposes an error of 0.27.; a is the angle between the incident laser beam\nand the reflecting fringe patterns.\n1.5-.\n\nThis angle can be determined to within\n\nUsing a typical value for a equal to 41-, an error of 37. is introduced\n\nfor the sin(a) term.\noMl and oM2\n\n1S\n\nThe error associated with measuring the fringe motion\n\nderived from the quality of the fringe patterns.\n\nWhen the\n\nfringes are bright and the speckles distributed smoothly, the fringes have\ngood quality and the analog signal is smooth - making it easy to identify a\n65\n\nFigure 32.\n\n66\n\nPhotomicrograph of Misplaced Indents. 50X.\n\nminimum.\n\nDuring the test, the indentations and their surrounding area can\n\nchange reflectivity which can make the speckles nonuniform and the analog signal ragged.\n\nThis behavior was found to be independent of strain level; there-\n\nfore, the possible error should not be thought of as a percent error, but\nrather, as an error band.\n\nThis value was determined from data collected over\n\na 2-hour duration at 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F) with no load applied to the specimen.\nThe drift of the measuring system was also established from these data.\nure 33 presents these data with the resulting error band evident.\nwidth is 300 microstrain.\nmicros train.\n\nFig-\n\nThe band\n\nThe total drift recorded was approximately -250\n\nIn conclusion, the relative uncertainty of the ISDG measured\n\nstrain values 1S determined to be \xc2\xb134, with an additional uncertainty of \xc2\xb1150\nmicros train.\n\nThe first uncertainty comes from the calibration constant, and\n\nthe second comes from uncertainty in locating the fringe m1n1mums.\n\nFor exam-\n\nple, a measured strain of 1.5% would have an uncertainty of 450 + 150 microstrain or \xc2\xb1600 microstrain total.\n\n67\n\nI\n\nI In. =1600".\n\n,.\n1 In.\n\n\xc2\xb71\n\n= 100 lie\n\n-\xc2\xab .\'t,~ .... t"~~ f~ ~l~ t.i1cl\'."~~~;.\\l.:~{\'fi"y"fr.\\f~\\ij:\'i~\\ . ~f1;~;r.;1 tdilH~(~"~"-W~~;r; through 66 depict the\n\nThe 1000 cycles of continuous cycle loading\n\nimposed on the specimen in Test No. 11A seemed to shorten the test life of\nthe specimen considerably.\n\nThe strain growth rate observed in Test No. llB\n\nwas almost twice as great as that detected in Test No.9.\n\nIt should be noted\n\nhere that the slight offset evident at the completion of Test No. l1A is not\ncontained in pattern.\n\nPlots of the loop\n\narea versus cycle for Test Nos. 11Aand 11B are smooth, Figures 67 and 68.\nA SEM photomicrograph of the notch region at the completion of the tests displays small surface cracks beginning to form, Figure 69.\n5.3\n\nTEST DATA DOCUMENTATION\nThe data collected in this program are available on a DEC RLOl hard disk.\n\nTable IX lists the order of appearance of Halford\n\nASSESSMENT OF VALIDITY OF MEASUREMENTS\nIn o.rder to ensure the validity of the results obtained from the benchmark\n\ntests, a room temperature calibration for each notched specimen was conducted\nprior to the actual testing of the specimen.\n\nThe calibration tests were made\n\nusing the ISDG to measure strain at the notch root of a specimen corresponding\nto loads well within the elastic limit of Inconel 718.\nof data were collected.\n\nTypically, 20 cycles\n\nThe slope of a linear regression of the average of\n\nthese cycles, multiplied by the stress concentration factor for the notch geometry, Kt = 1.9, was used to determine a room temperature elastic modulus, E,\nfor the specimen.\nThe initial elastic modulus of each specimen at 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F) was also\nestablished.\n\nThis value was determined from the linear portion of the initial\n\ntest cycle.\n\nThe elastic moduli of elasticity as measured at both temperatures\n\nfor each specimen are presented ln Table X.\n\nIII\n\nThe modulus of elasticity of Inconel 718 bar stock as measured in the\npedigree testing was presented in Table VII.\nare within 1.7\n\nsta~dard\n\nThe moduli measured by the ISDG\n\ndeviations of the mean measured pedigree values at\n\n649- C.\nSince Test \xc2\xb0Nos. 6 and llA are identical, a direct comparison of the\nmeasured data can be made.\ncycles of each test.\n\nFigures 70 and 71 compare the 1st and 1000th\n\nA very good correlation is noted, thus lending added\n\nconfidence to the repeatability of the measurement program.\nIn conclusion, it is felt that the ISDG performed within the previously\nestablished relative uncertainty of \xc2\xb137. with an additional uncertainty of\n\xc2\xb1150 micros train during the benchmark test program.\n112\n\n"\nt.:)\n\n...\n\no\n\no\n\nTest 6\n\ni:!. is currently the most widely used method\n\nin the industry for solution of complex stress analysis problems.\n\nAlthough\n\ndetailed FEM analyses were not conducted for this program, FEM studies were\ndone in several cases for comparison purposes.\nThe computer\n\np~ogram\n\nemployed is the plane stress version of the elastic-\n\nplastic-creep finite-element program CYANIDE.\n\nThe program utilizes the method\n\nof subvolumes as described in detail in References 26 and 33.\nprogram employs constant strain\n\n~isYmmetric\n\nBriefly, the\n\ntriangular elements and the\n\nmethod of subvolumes to introduce plastic flow and hardening characteristics.\nThis nonlinear FEM solves the system of equations:\n[K)\n\n(q)\n\n=\n\n( 12)\n\n(p)\n\nwhere:\n[K] is the stiffness matrix\n(q)\n\n1S\n\nthe nodal point displacement vector\n\n(p) is the nodal point force vector.\nThe solution is obtained by iterative perturbations of the right-hand side\nof Equation 12 through the addition of a plastic pseudoforce vector (P p ):\n(13)\n\nThis procedure is more economical than the more conventional iterative\nrevision of the stiffness matrix K.\ndepict the\n\nno~linea~\n\nThe plasticity theory which is used to\n\ncyclic behavior is an extension of the one proposed by\n\nBesseling (Reference 34).\nThe model is capable of simulating the Bauschinger effect of reduced,\ncyclic yield stress and cross-hardening, two effects considered to be\nimportant in cyc lically loaded struc tures.\n\n.\n\nThe computer code solutions have been verified elastically through\ncorrelations with known closed-form solutions and photoelastic tests.\n\nStress\n\nand strain solutions involving plasticity and creep have been indirectly verified through correlations obtained between predictions and observations of low\ncycle fatigue and crack propagation test results (References 26 and 33).\n122\n\n6.3\n\nNEUBER PREDICTION OF NOTCH ROOT STRESS AND STRAIN\nUsing Equation 10, analysis that the basic behavior of the creep\n\nresponse was not affected by the load step changes.\n\n140\n\n7.0\n\n7.1\n\nTASK V - COMPARISON OF EXPERIMENTAL VERSUS ANALYTICAL RESULTS\n\nNEUBER ANALYSIS VERSUS EXPERIMENTAL RESULTS\nComparisons between the measured notch root strain responses and the\n\nNeuber predictions (based on the monotonic stress-strain relationship only)\nare made schematically 1n Figures 84 through 89 for the first complete cycle\nof each load pattern.\n\nIn all cases, since there is relatively little initial\n\nplasticity, the Neuber method has predicted local strain very well.\n\nOne ques-\n\ntion addressed in Section 6.1 is the Neuber model\'s inability to handle stress\nredistribution when the plasticity at the\' notch root becomes large.\n\nUsing the\n\nmodified Neuber analysis described 1n that section, the prediction is shown in\nFigure 90.\n\nIt is unclear from these data if this represents an improved pre-\n\ndiction.\nSignificant scatter in the strain measurement was observed during the\n2-minute hold time, which precludes an accurate measurement of the creep\nstrain.\n\n(This is consistent with the predicted low creep strain during this\n\nhold.)\n\nHowever, the creep deformation could be observed clearly in the multi-\n\nstep creep test (Test No. 12) which had longer hold time in each load step.\nThe initial plastic strain 1S well predicted.\n\nHowever, during the first hold\n\nperiod the measured s train decreases while the predic ted strain increases.\nThis phenomenon occurred at several of the load increments.\n\nAlthough the\n\nexplanation for this is unclear, the overall trend of the predicted creep\nstrain agrees fairly well with the test data.\nSeveral intermediate cycles which gradually approach the steady-state\ncyclic response of each load pattern were examined and compared with analytical\npredictions on the basis of null initial strain (Figures 91 through 97).\n\nThe\n\ntrend of increasing hysteresis loop size as cycling proceeds indicates the\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nsoftening behavior of the material.\n\nThese figures also illustrate monotonic/\n\ncyclic transition of material behavior.\n\nIn Test Nos. 7 and lIB (shown in\n\nFigures 92 and 97), decreases of the elastic loading slope, which is indicative of the stiffness of the specimen, were observed in the final cycles.\nThis is indicative of the formation and propagation of cracks at the notch\nroot.\n141\n\n= 1. 90\n\nI\n\nX ISDG the test results\nAt maximum loads, the transitional changes of the\n\nmeasured stress from the monotonic to cyclic predictions were successfully\npredicted.\n\nHowever, at unloaded points the Neuber analysis, based on the\n\nmonotonic stress-strain relationship, predicts higher compressive stress than\nthat measured in initial cycles.\n\nIn both cases, the Neuber notch root stress\n\nprediction using the cyclic stress-strain relationship is in excellent agreement with the stabilized stresses measured in the smooth bar test.\n\nFor com-\n\nparison purposes, the finite-element solution (based on stress-strain relationships in Equations 14 and 15) is also shown in the figure.\n\nIt is important to\n\nnote that stabilization of the cyclic softening would not have been complete\nin this test (refer to Figure 42).\n\nThe finite-element solution would be\n\napproached as softening continued.\n\nThese results may imply that the Massing\n\n(or kinematic hardening) hypothesis, addressed in Section 6.1, is suited for\ncyclically stable material behavior only.\n7.2\n\nFINITE-ELEMENT ANALYSIS VERSUS EXPERIMENTAL RESULTS\nStrain analysis using the finite-element method was conducted for least squares\nmethod.\n\nThe slope, which corresponds to the modulus of the material and the\n\nstiffness of the specimen, could be scrutinized against the elapsed cycles to\noversee the transient changes of material or structural parameters.\n\nAs an\n\nexample, Figure 101 represents the loading and unloading slopes versus cycles\nof a continuous cycle test (Test No. 7)*.\nslightly at the 0\n\n400\n\n--r----L----r----L----r---I\nI\nI\nI\n,\n200\n\nCYClE\n\nCumclative Creep Strain in the Cycle History of\nTest 10.\n\n450\n\n8.0\n\nDISCUSSION OF RESULTS\n\nThe Benchmark Notch Program was successfully completed with the generation\nof substantial quantities of strain measurement data for a notched geometry,\nNi-base superalloy specimen at elevated temperature.\n\nA standard Neuber anal-\n\nysis can be used for the notch root stress-strain determination with reasonable\nengineering accuracy for initial cyc les.\n\nHowever, if the specimen has a high\n\ndegree of plasticity, some adjustment for stress redistribution may be required\n,j\n\nto improve the solution.\nFor later cycles, the local notch root stress and strain did not approach\nthat predicted using the cyclic stress-strain curve.\nthe distribution of plastic ity near the notch.\n\nThis is probably due to\n\nThe notch surface would tend\n\nto approach the stable cyclic curve; however, the surrounding material is not\nas highly strained and converges to a different stable condition and so on as\none moves away from the notch.\n\nThis constraint behavior must be more closely\n\nmodelled in order to achieve correlation with the measured results.\nThe 2-minute hold period adopted in the hold-time tests was found to be\ninsufficient for detailed creep strain measurement.\n\nLonger hold time should\n\nbe used to enhance the measurement of creep strain and verify the effect of\ncyclic load on the creep behavior.\n\nHowever, a modified Neuber prediction that\n\nincludes creep strain and stress relaxation in\n\nt~e\n\nanalysis was shown to be in\n\ngood agreement with the general trends of the measured results from the multistep creep test.\n\nInitial strain decreases following load-step changes remain\n\nunexplained.\nThe finite-element method resulted in a better notch root solution due to\nthe improved treatment of stress redistribution, but the finite-element solution using the cyclic stress-strain curve also failed to correlate the higher\ncycle results.\n\nFor a better simulation, the material properties of the finite-\n\nelement model should be properly chosen for selected areas.\nThe computer-based data acquisition system facilitated the process of\nexperimental data collection and documentation.\n\nThis strongly suggests\n\ncomputerized testing procedures should be adopted in more laboratories.\n\n176\n\nThe\n\nadditional observations of certain parameters highlight the insight to be\ngained by scanning the entire test history.\n\nThese same parameters may also\n\nprovide information for fatigue life prediction, although further exploration\nin this area is needed \xe2\x80\xa2\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n177\n\n9.0\n\nCONCLUSIONS\n\nThe following summary conclusions are drawn from this study.\nBenchmark notch root strain measurements were made and documented for\ns~x\n\nload patterns representative of typical aircraft engine cycles on a\n\nnickel-base superalloy at elevated temperature.\nThe laser interferometric strain displacement gage (ISDG) measuring\nsystem was demonstrated to be adaptable to continuous notch strain measurement at 649\xc2\xb7 C (1200\xc2\xb7 F) on Inconel 718.\nThe ISDG performed within the relative uncertainty of \xc2\xb1\n\n3~\n\nwith an\n\nadditional uncertainty of \xc2\xb1 150 micros train for continuous and hold-time\ntests.\nThe computer-based data acquisition system\ntory experimental programs.\n\n~s\n\na versatile tool for labora-\n\nIt facilitates the process of data gathering,\n\nretrieval, and manipulation.\nThe double-notched, plane stress, Kt = 1.9, buttonhead test specimen is\nwell suited to this type of experimental strain measurement system. No major\nexperimental problems were encountered in its use.\nNeuber-based analysis can be used to determine notch root stress and\nstrain within engineering accuracy for monotonic loading.\n\nModifications of\n\nthe Neuber analysis to account for creep behavior successfully predicted measured local creep strain trends.\nIn the Neuber analysis, the importance of using strain rate dependent\nmaterial properties was demonstrated.\nThe Neuber predictions of stable local notch root stress-strain hysteresis loops, based on appropriate strain rate cyclic stress-strain curves, did not,\nin general, agree with test results.\n\nIn some tests, complete stabilization may\n\nnot have been achieved; however, this lack of correlation was also observed\n~n\n\ntests where essentially complete softening had occurred.\nA limited elastic-plastic finite-element analysis improved the calcula-\n\ntions, but did not totally alleviate the observed differences.\n\n178\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\nThe monotonic and\n\ncyclic~axLmum\n\nstresses,\n\nan~\n\nthe cyclic mLnlmum stresses\n\nwere, however, well predicted by the Neuber apprQach while early cyclic minimum\nstresses were not.\n\nThis suggests that the kinematic-hardening assumptions\n\nmay be inappropriate for the early transitional behavior.\nThe 2-minute period used in the hold-time test was found to be inadequate for measurement of creep strain and verification of the basic behavior\nof the fatigue-creep interaction.\n\nPersistent creep did occur, however, and\n\ncould be assessed by calculating cumulative creep strain from the ISDG\nmeasurements.\nScrutinizing varlOUS testing and material parameters, such as hysteresis\nloop width, loop area, etc., has provided understanding of the transitional\nchanges in material behavior and response throughout the entire testing\nhistory.\nThe loading modulus parameter provides a convenient means of determining\nnotch root crack formation while the hysteresis loop area parameter clearly\ndefines stabilization .\n\n\xe2\x80\xa2\n\n179\n\nREFERENCES\n\n1.\n\nMerrill, P.S., "Photodot Investigation of Plastic-Strain Pattern in
